Military
--------

[![Image 61](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/RSD-10_2009_G1.jpg/250px-RSD-10_2009_G1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RSD-10_2009_G1.jpg)

A medium-range [SS-20](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RSD-10 "RSD-10") non-[ICBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ICBM "ICBM") ballistic missile, the deployment of which by the Soviet Union in the late 1970s launched a new [arms race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_race "Arms race") in Europe when NATO responded by deploying [Pershing II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pershing_II "Pershing II") missiles in [West Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Germany "West Germany"), among other things

Under the Military Law of September 1925, the [Soviet Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Armed_Forces "Soviet Armed Forces") consisted of the [Land Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Army "Soviet Army"), the [Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Air_Forces "Soviet Air Forces"), the [Navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Navy "Soviet Navy"), [Joint State Political Directorate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint_State_Political_Directorate "Joint State Political Directorate") (OGPU) and the [Internal Troops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internal_Troops "Internal Troops").[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-119) The OGPU later became independent and in 1934 joined the [NKVD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NKVD "NKVD") secret police, and so its internal troops were under the joint leadership of the defense and internal commissariats. After World War II, [Strategic Missile Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Missile_Forces "Strategic Missile Forces") (1959), [Air Defense Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Air_Defence_Forces "Soviet Air Defence Forces") (1948) and National Civil Defense Forces (1970) were formed, which ranked first, third, and sixth in the official Soviet system of importance (ground forces were second, Air Force fourth, and Navy fifth).

The army had the greatest political influence. In 1989, there served two million soldiers divided between 150 motorized and 52 armored divisions. Until the early 1960s, the Soviet navy was a rather small military branch, but after the [Caribbean crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean_crisis "Caribbean crisis"), under the leadership of [Sergei Gorshkov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergei_Gorshkov "Sergei Gorshkov"), it expanded significantly. It became known for [battlecruisers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battlecruiser "Battlecruiser") and submarines. In 1989, there served 500 000 men. The [Soviet Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Air_Force "Soviet Air Force") focused on a fleet of [strategic bombers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_bomber "Strategic bomber") and during war situation was to eradicate enemy infrastructure and nuclear capacity. The air force also had a number of [fighters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fighter_aircraft "Fighter aircraft") and tactical bombers to support the army in the war. Strategic missile forces had more than 1,400 [intercontinental ballistic missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile") (ICBMs), deployed between 28 bases and 300 command centers.

In the post-war period, the Soviet Army was directly involved in several military operations abroad.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-GDP-21)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-120)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-121) These included the suppression of the [uprising in East Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_German_uprising_of_1953 "East German uprising of 1953") (1953), [Hungarian revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956 "Hungarian Revolution of 1956") (1956) and the [invasion of Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Czechoslovakia "Invasion of Czechoslovakia") (1968). The Soviet Union also participated in the [war in Afghanistan between 1979 and 1989](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War").

In the Soviet Union, general [conscription](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conscription "Conscription") applied, meaning all able-bodied males aged 18 and older were drafted in the armed forces.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-122)

Economy
-------

[![Image 62](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Gdp_per_capita_1965.png/330px-Gdp_per_capita_1965.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gdp_per_capita_1965.png)

The Soviet Union in comparison to other countries by GDP (nominal) per capita in 1965 based on a West-German school book (1971)[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]
> 5,000 [DM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsche_Mark "Deutsche Mark")

2,500–5,000 [DM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsche_Mark "Deutsche Mark")

1,000–2,500 [DM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsche_Mark "Deutsche Mark")500–1,000 [DM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsche_Mark "Deutsche Mark")

250–500 [DM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsche_Mark "Deutsche Mark")

The Soviet Union adopted a [command economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Command_economy "Command economy"), whereby production and distribution of goods were centralized and directed by the government. For the overwhelming majority of its existence, the USSR did not use GDP or GNP to measure its economy, instead relying on the [Material Product System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Material_Product_System "Material Product System"). The first Bolshevik experience with a command economy was the policy of [war communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_communism "War communism"), which involved the nationalization of industry, centralized distribution of output, coercive or forced requisition of agricultural production, and attempts to eliminate money circulation, private enterprises and [free trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_trade "Free trade"). The [barrier troops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barrier_troops "Barrier troops") were also used to enforce Bolshevik control over food supplies in areas controlled by the Red Army, a role which soon earned them the hatred of the Russian civilian population.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-123) After the severe economic collapse, Lenin replaced war [communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communism "Communism") by the [New Economic Policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Economic_Policy "New Economic Policy") (NEP) in 1921, legalizing free trade and private ownership of small businesses. The economy steadily recovered as a result.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Gregory-2004-124)

After a long debate among the members of the Politburo about the course of economic development, by 1928–1929, upon gaining control of the country, Stalin abandoned the NEP and pushed for full central planning, starting [forced collectivization of agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collectivization_in_the_Soviet_Union "Collectivization in the Soviet Union") and enacting draconian labour legislation. Resources were mobilized for [rapid industrialization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Soviet_Union_(1927%E2%80%9353)#Industrialization_in_practice "History of the Soviet Union (1927–53)"), which significantly expanded Soviet capacity in heavy industry and capital goods during the 1930s.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Gregory-2004-124) The primary motivation for industrialization was preparation for war, mostly due to distrust of the outside capitalist world.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-125) As a result, the USSR was transformed from a largely agrarian economy into a great industrial power, leading the way for its emergence as a superpower after [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II").[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-126) The war caused extensive devastation of the Soviet economy and infrastructure, which required massive reconstruction.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-127)

[![Image 63](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/DneproGES_1947.JPG/250px-DneproGES_1947.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:DneproGES_1947.JPG)

The [DneproGES](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dnieper_Hydroelectric_Station "Dnieper Hydroelectric Station"), one of many [hydroelectric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroelectric "Hydroelectric") power stations in the Soviet Union

By the early 1940s, the Soviet economy had become relatively [self-sufficient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autarky "Autarky"); for most of the period until the creation of [Comecon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comecon "Comecon"), only a tiny share of domestic products was traded internationally.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Library_of_Congress_Country_Studies-2-128) After the creation of the Eastern Bloc, external trade rose rapidly. However, the influence of the [world economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_economy "World economy") on the USSR was limited by fixed domestic prices and a state monopoly on [foreign trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_trade_of_the_Soviet_Union "Foreign trade of the Soviet Union").[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-129) Grain and sophisticated consumer manufactures became major import articles from around the 1960s.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Library_of_Congress_Country_Studies-2-128) During the [arms race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_race "Arms race") of the Cold War, the Soviet economy was burdened by military expenditures, heavily lobbied for by a powerful bureaucracy dependent on the arms industry. At the same time, the USSR became the largest arms exporter to the [Third World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_World "Third World"). A portion of Soviet resources during the Cold War were [allocated in aid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_relations_within_the_Comecon "International relations within the Comecon") to the Soviet-aligned states.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Library_of_Congress_Country_Studies-2-128) The Soviet Union's [military budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_budget "Military budget") in the 1970s was gigantic, forming 40–60% of the entire federal budget and accounting to 15% of the USSR's GDP (13% in the 1980s).[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-130)

[![Image 64](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Armenian_cotton.jpg/250px-Armenian_cotton.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Armenian_cotton.jpg)

Picking cotton in [Armenia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic "Armenian Soviet Socialist Republic") in the 1930s

From the 1930s until its dissolution in late 1991, the way the Soviet economy operated remained essentially unchanged. The economy was formally directed by [central planning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_planning "Economic planning"), carried out by [Gosplan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gosplan "Gosplan") and organized in [five-year plans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Five-year_plans_for_the_national_economy_of_the_Soviet_Union "Five-year plans for the national economy of the Soviet Union"). However, in practice, the plans were highly aggregated and provisional, subject to _ad hoc_ intervention by superiors. All critical economic decisions were taken by the political leadership. Allocated resources and plan targets were usually denominated in [rubles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_ruble "Soviet ruble") rather than in physical goods. [Credit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Credit "Credit") was discouraged, but widespread. The final allocation of output was achieved through relatively decentralized, unplanned contracting. Although in theory prices were legally set from above, in practice they were often negotiated, and informal horizontal links (e.g. between producer factories) were widespread.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Gregory-2004-124)

A number of basic [services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Service_(economics) "Service (economics)") were state-funded, such as [education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_the_Soviet_Union "Education in the Soviet Union") and health care. In the manufacturing sector, heavy industry and defence were prioritized over [consumer goods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consumer_goods_in_the_Soviet_Union "Consumer goods in the Soviet Union").[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Library_of_Congress_Country_Studies-131) Consumer goods, particularly outside large cities, were often scarce, of poor quality and limited variety. Under the command economy, consumers had almost no influence on production, and the changing demands of a population with growing incomes could not be satisfied by supplies at rigidly fixed prices.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Hanson-132) A massive unplanned second economy grew up at low levels alongside the planned one, providing some of the goods and services that the planners could not. The legalization of some elements of the decentralized economy was attempted with the [reform of 1965](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1965_Soviet_economic_reform "1965 Soviet economic reform").[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Gregory-2004-124)

[![Image 65](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/RIAN_archive_633872_Workers_of_Soligorsk_potash_plant.jpg/250px-RIAN_archive_633872_Workers_of_Soligorsk_potash_plant.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_633872_Workers_of_Soligorsk_potash_plant.jpg)

Workers of the [Salihorsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salihorsk "Salihorsk") potash plant, [Belarus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic "Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic"), 1968

Although statistics of the Soviet economy are notoriously unreliable and its economic growth difficult to estimate precisely,[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-133)[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-134) by most accounts, the economy continued to expand until the mid-1980s. During the 1950s and 1960s, it had comparatively high growth and was catching up to the West.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-135) However, after 1970, the growth, while still positive, [steadily declined](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Era_of_Stagnation "Era of Stagnation") much more quickly and consistently than in other countries, despite a rapid increase in the capital [stock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stock "Stock") (the rate of capital increase was only surpassed by Japan).[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Gregory-2004-124)

[![Image 66](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/RIAN_archive_878967_AvtoVAZ-_Volga_automaking_plant_in_Togliatti%2C_the_Samara_Region.jpg/250px-RIAN_archive_878967_AvtoVAZ-_Volga_automaking_plant_in_Togliatti%2C_the_Samara_Region.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_878967_AvtoVAZ-_Volga_automaking_plant_in_Togliatti,_the_Samara_Region.jpg)

[Volzhsky Avtomobilny Zavod](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AvtoVAZ "AvtoVAZ") (VAZ) in 1969

Overall, the growth rate of per capita income in the Soviet Union between 1960 and 1989 was slightly above the world average (based on 102 countries).[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischerEasterly19944-136) A 1986 study published in the _[American Journal of Public Health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American\_Journal\_of\_Public\_Health "American Journal of Public Health")_ claimed that, citing [World Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") data, the Soviet model provided a better [quality of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_of_life "Quality of life") and [human development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_development_(economics) "Human development (economics)") than market economies at the same level of economic development in most cases.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-137) According to [Stanley Fischer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanley_Fischer "Stanley Fischer") and [William Easterly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Easterly "William Easterly"), growth could have been faster. By their calculation, per capita income in 1989 should have been twice higher than it was, considering the amount of investment, education and population. The authors attribute this poor performance to the low productivity of capital.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischerEasterly19945-138) Steven Rosefielde states that the [standard of living](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_of_living "Standard of living") declined due to Stalin's despotism. While there was a brief improvement after his death, it lapsed into stagnation.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-139)

In 1987, [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev") attempted to reform and revitalize the economy with his program of _[perestroika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perestroika "Perestroika")_. His policies relaxed state control over enterprises but did not replace it by market incentives, resulting in a sharp decline in output. The economy, already suffering from [reduced petroleum export revenues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1980s_oil_glut "1980s oil glut"), started to collapse. Prices were still fixed, and the property was still largely state-owned until after the country's dissolution.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Gregory-2004-124)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Hanson-132) For most of the period after World War II until its collapse, Soviet GDP ([PPP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity "Purchasing power parity")) was [the second-largest in the world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_regions_by_past_GDP_(PPP) "List of regions by past GDP (PPP)"), and third during the second half of the 1980s,[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-140) although on a [per-capita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)_per_capita "List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita") basis, it was behind that of [First World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_World "First World") countries.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-141) Compared to countries with similar per-capita GDP in 1928, the Soviet Union experienced significant growth.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-142)

In 1990, the country had a [Human Development Index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Development_Index "Human Development Index") of 0.920, placing it in the 'high' category of human development. It was the third-highest in the Eastern Bloc, behind [Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czechoslovak_Socialist_Republic "Czechoslovak Socialist Republic") and [East Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Germany "East Germany"), and the 25th in the world of 130 countries.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-143)

### Energy

[![Image 67](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1987_CPA_5858.jpg/250px-1987_CPA_5858.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1987_CPA_5858.jpg)

A Soviet stamp depicting the 30th anniversary of the [International Atomic Energy Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency "International Atomic Energy Agency"), published in 1987, a year following the [Chernobyl nuclear disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chernobyl_nuclear_disaster "Chernobyl nuclear disaster")

The need for fuel declined in the Soviet Union from the 1970s to the 1980s,[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983105%E2%80%93108-144) both per ruble of gross social product and per ruble of industrial product. The decline was very rapid between 1965 and 1970, then slowed between 1970 and 1975. From 1975 to 1980, the decline continued at an even slower rate, with fuel requirements per ruble of gross social product decreasing by only 2.6%.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983295-145) David Wilson, a historian, believed that the gas industry would account for 40% of Soviet fuel production by the end of the century. His theory did not come to fruition because of the USSR's collapse.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983297-146) According to Wilson, the Soviet Union was, in theory, well-positioned to avoid an energy crisis and could have sustained economic growth rates of 2–2.5% during the 1990s, supported by its energy resources.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983297%E2%80%93299-147) However, the energy sector faced many difficulties, among them the country's high military expenditure and hostile relations with the [First World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_World "First World").[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983299-148)

In 1991, the Soviet Union had a [pipeline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pipeline "Pipeline") network of 82,000 kilometres (51,000 mi) for [crude oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crude_oil "Crude oil") and another 206,500 kilometres (128,300 mi) for natural gas.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Central_Intelligence_Agency-1991-149) Petroleum and petroleum-based products, natural gas, metals, wood, agricultural products, and a variety of manufactured goods, primarily machinery, arms and military equipment, were exported.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-150) In the 1970s and 1980s, the USSR heavily relied on fossil fuel exports to earn [hard currency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hard_currency "Hard currency").[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Library_of_Congress_Country_Studies-2-128) At its peak in 1988, it was the largest producer and second-largest exporter of crude oil, surpassed only by [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia").[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-151)

### Science and technology

[![Image 68](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Sputnik-stamp-ussr.jpg/250px-Sputnik-stamp-ussr.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sputnik-stamp-ussr.jpg)

Soviet stamp showing the orbit of [Sputnik 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputnik_1 "Sputnik 1")

The Soviet Union placed great emphasis on [science and technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_the_Soviet_Union "Science and technology in the Soviet Union").[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-152)[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-153) Lenin believed the USSR would never overtake the developed world if it remained as technologically backward as it was upon its founding. Soviet authorities proved their commitment to Lenin's belief by developing massive networks and research and development organizations. In the early 1960s, 40% of chemistry PhDs in the Soviet Union were attained by women, compared with only 5% in the United States.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-154) By 1989, Soviet scientists were among the world's best-trained specialists in several areas, such as energy physics, selected areas of medicine, mathematics, welding, space technology, and military technologies. However, due to rigid state planning and [bureaucracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nomenklatura "Nomenklatura"), the Soviets remained far behind the [First World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_World "First World") in chemistry, biology, and computer science. Under Stalin, the Soviet government persecuted [geneticists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geneticists "Geneticists") in favour of [Lysenkoism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lysenkoism "Lysenkoism"), a [pseudoscience](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudoscience "Pseudoscience") rejected by the scientific community in the Soviet Union and abroad but supported by Stalin's inner circles. Implemented in the USSR and China, it resulted in reduced crop yields and is widely believed to have contributed to the [Great Chinese Famine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Chinese_Famine "Great Chinese Famine").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-155) In the 1980s, the Soviet Union had more [scientists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientists "Scientists") and [engineers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engineers "Engineers") relative to the world's population than any other major country, owing to strong levels of state support.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-156) Some of its most remarkable technological achievements, such as launching the [world's first space satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputnik_1 "Sputnik 1"), were achieved through military research.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Library_of_Congress_Country_Studies-131)

Under the [Reagan administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reagan_administration "Reagan administration"), [Project Socrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_Socrates "Project Socrates") determined that the Soviet Union addressed the acquisition of science and technology in a manner radically different to the United States. The US prioritized indigenous [research and development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Research_and_development "Research and development") in both the public and private sectors. In contrast, the USSR placed greater emphasis on acquiring foreign technology, which it did through both [covert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrial_espionage "Industrial espionage") and overt means. However, centralized state planning kept Soviet technological development greatly inflexible. This was exploited by the US to undermine the strength of the Soviet Union and thus foster its reform.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-157)[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-158)[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-159)

### Space program

[![Image 69](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/RIAN_archive_159271_Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Valentina_Tereshkova%2C_Pavel_Popovich_and_Yury_Gagarin_at_Lenin_Mausoleum.jpg/250px-RIAN_archive_159271_Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Valentina_Tereshkova%2C_Pavel_Popovich_and_Yury_Gagarin_at_Lenin_Mausoleum.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_159271_Nikita_Khrushchev,_Valentina_Tereshkova,_Pavel_Popovich_and_Yury_Gagarin_at_Lenin_Mausoleum.jpg)

From left to right: [Yuri Gagarin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin "Yuri Gagarin"), [Pavel Popovich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pavel_Popovich "Pavel Popovich"), [Valentina Tereshkova](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova "Valentina Tereshkova") and [Nikita Khrushchev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev "Nikita Khrushchev") at the [Lenin's Mausoleum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin%27s_Mausoleum "Lenin's Mausoleum") in 1963

[![Image 70](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Soyuz_18_booster.jpg/250px-Soyuz_18_booster.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Soyuz_18_booster.jpg)

[Soyuz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_(rocket_family) "Soyuz (rocket family)") rocket at the [Baikonur Cosmodrome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome "Baikonur Cosmodrome")

At the end of the 1950s, the USSR constructed the first [satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite "Satellite")—[Sputnik 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputnik_1 "Sputnik 1"), which marked the beginning of the [Space Race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Race "Space Race")—a competition to achieve superior spaceflight capability with the United States.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-160) This was followed by other successful satellites, most notably [Sputnik 5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputnik_5 "Sputnik 5"), where test dogs were sent to space. On 12 April 1961, the USSR launched [Vostok 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vostok_1 "Vostok 1"), which carried [Yuri Gagarin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin "Yuri Gagarin"), making him the first human to ever be launched into space and complete a space journey.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-161) The first plans for [space shuttles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaceplane "Spaceplane") and orbital stations were drawn up in Soviet design offices, but personal disputes between designers and management prevented their development.

In terms of the [Luna program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luna_program "Luna program"), the USSR only had automated spacecraft launches with no crewed spacecraft, passing on the 'Moon' part of [Space Race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Race "Space Race"), which was [won by the Americans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_11 "Apollo 11"). The Soviet public's reaction to the American moon-landing was mixed. The Soviet government limited the release of information about it, which affected the reaction. A portion of the populace did not give it attention, and another portion was angered.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-162)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-163)

In the 1970s, specific proposals for the design of a space shuttle emerged, but shortcomings, especially in the electronics industry (rapid overheating of electronics), postponed it till the end of the 1980s. The first shuttle, the [Buran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buran_(spacecraft) "Buran (spacecraft)"), flew in 1988, but without a human crew. Another, _[Ptichka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ptichka "Ptichka")_, endured prolonged construction and was canceled in 1991. For their launch into space, there is today an unused superpower rocket, [Energia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energia_(rocket) "Energia (rocket)"), which is the most powerful in the world.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-164)

In the late 1980s, the Soviet Union built the _[Mir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir "Mir")_ orbital station. It was built on the construction of [_Salyut_ stations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salyut_programme "Salyut programme") and its only role was civilian-grade research tasks.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Harland-2020-165)[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-166) Mir was the only orbital station in operation from 1986 to 1998. Gradually, other modules were added to it, including American modules. However, the station deteriorated rapidly after a fire on board and was deorbited in 2001, burning up in the Earth's atmosphere.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Harland-2020-165)

### Transport

[![Image 71](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flag_of_the_Aeroflot.svg/250px-Flag_of_the_Aeroflot.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Aeroflot.svg)

[Aeroflot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aeroflot "Aeroflot")'s flag during the Soviet era

[![Image 72](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/RUS-2016-Murmansk-Icebreaker_Lenin_01.jpg/250px-RUS-2016-Murmansk-Icebreaker_Lenin_01.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RUS-2016-Murmansk-Icebreaker_Lenin_01.jpg)

Nuclear Icebreaker _[Lenin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin\_(1957\_icebreaker) "Lenin (1957 icebreaker)")_

Transport was a vital component of the country's economy. The [economic centralization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_five-year_plan_(Soviet_Union) "First five-year plan (Soviet Union)") of the late 1920s and 1930s led to the development of infrastructure on a massive scale, most notably the establishment of [Aeroflot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aeroflot "Aeroflot"), an aviation enterprise.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-167) The country had a wide variety of modes of transport by land, water and air.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Central_Intelligence_Agency-1991-149) However, due to inadequate maintenance, much of the road, water and Soviet civil aviation transport were outdated and technologically backward compared to the First World.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983205-168)

Soviet rail transport was the largest and most intensively used in the world;[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983205-168) it was also better developed than most of its Western counterparts.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1983201-169) By the late 1970s and early 1980s, Soviet economists were calling for the construction of more roads to alleviate some of the burdens from the railways and to improve the Soviet [government budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_budget "Government budget").[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmblerShawSymons1985166%E2%80%93167-170) The [street network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Street_network "Street network") and [automotive industry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_the_Soviet_Union "Automotive industry in the Soviet Union")[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmblerShawSymons1985168-171) remained underdeveloped,[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmblerShawSymons1985165-172) and [dirt roads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirt_road "Dirt road") were common outside major cities.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmblerShawSymons1985167-173) Soviet maintenance projects proved unable to take care of even the few roads the country had. By the early-to-mid-1980s, the Soviet authorities tried to solve the road problem by ordering the construction of new ones.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmblerShawSymons1985167-173) Meanwhile, the automobile industry was growing at a faster rate than road construction.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmblerShawSymons1985169-174) The underdeveloped road network led to a growing demand for public transport.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-175)

Despite improvements, several aspects of the transport sector were still[_[when?](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Dates\_and\_numbers#Chronological\_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_] riddled with problems due to outdated infrastructure, lack of investment, corruption and bad decision-making. Soviet authorities were unable to meet the growing demand for transport infrastructure and services.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-176)

The Soviet [merchant navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merchant_navy "Merchant navy") was one of the largest in the world.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Central_Intelligence_Agency-1991-149)

Demographics
------------

[![Image 73](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Population_of_former_USSR.PNG/330px-Population_of_former_USSR.PNG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Population_of_former_USSR.PNG)

Population of the Soviet Union (red) and the [post-Soviet states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-Soviet_states "Post-Soviet states") (blue) from 1961 to 2009 as well as projection (dotted blue) from 2010 to 2100

Excess deaths throughout [World War I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I "World War I") and the [Russian Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Civil_War "Russian Civil War") (including the [famine of 1921–1922](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_famine_of_1921%E2%80%931922 "Russian famine of 1921–1922") that was triggered by Lenin's [war communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_communism "War communism") policies)[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-177) amounted to a combined total of 18 million,[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-178) some 10 million in the 1930s,[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-179) and more than 20 million in 1941–1945. The postwar [Soviet population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_of_the_Soviet_Union "Population of the Soviet Union") was 45 to 50 million smaller than it would have been if pre-war demographic growth had continued.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-180) According to [Catherine Merridale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catherine_Merridale "Catherine Merridale"), '...reasonable estimate would place the total number of excess deaths for the whole period somewhere around 60 million.'[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-181)

The [birth rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birth_rate "Birth rate") of the USSR decreased from 44.0 per thousand in 1926 to 18.0 in 1974, mainly due to increasing urbanization and the rising average age of marriages. The [mortality rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mortality_rate "Mortality rate") demonstrated a gradual decrease as well—from 23.7 per thousand in 1926 to 8.7 in 1974. In general, the birth rates of the southern republics in Transcaucasia and Central Asia were considerably higher than those in the northern parts of the Soviet Union, and in some cases even increased in the post–World War II period, a phenomenon partly attributed to slower rates of urbanization and traditionally earlier marriages in the southern republics.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-182) Soviet Europe moved towards [sub-replacement fertility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility "Sub-replacement fertility"), while [Soviet Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Central_Asia "Soviet Central Asia") continued to exhibit population growth well above replacement-level fertility.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-183)

The late 1960s and the 1970s witnessed a reversal of the declining trajectory of the rate of mortality in the USSR, and was especially notable among men of working age, but was also prevalent in Russia and other predominantly Slavic areas of the country.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-184) An analysis of the official data from the late 1980s showed that after worsening in the late-1970s and the early 1980s, adult mortality began to improve again.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-185) The infant mortality rate increased from 24.7 in 1970 to 27.9 in 1974. Some researchers regarded the rise as mostly real, a consequence of worsening health conditions and services.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-186) The rises in both adult and infant mortality were not explained or defended by Soviet officials, and the [Soviet government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_government "Soviet government") stopped publishing all mortality statistics for ten years. Soviet demographers and health specialists remained silent about the mortality increases until the late-1980s, when the publication of mortality data resumed, and researchers could delve into the real causes.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-187)

### Urbanism

[![Image 74](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Largest_cities_USSR_1989.svg/250px-Largest_cities_USSR_1989.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Largest_cities_USSR_1989.svg)

Largest cities of the USSR according to the 1989 census

The Soviet Union imposed heavy controls on city growth, preventing some cities from reaching their full potential while promoting others.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-188)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Stronski-2010-189)

For the entirety of the Soviet Union's existence, the most populous cities were [Moscow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow "Moscow") and [Leningrad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leningrad "Leningrad") (both in [Russian SFSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_SFSR "Russian SFSR")), with the third far place taken by [Kiev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kiev "Kiev") ([Ukrainian SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainian_SSR "Ukrainian SSR")). At the USSR's inception, the fourth and fifth most populous cities were [Kharkov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kharkov "Kharkov") (Ukrainian SSR) and [Baku](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baku "Baku") ([Azerbaijan SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azerbaijan_SSR "Azerbaijan SSR")), but, by the end of the century, [Tashkent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tashkent "Tashkent") ([Uzbek SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uzbek_SSR "Uzbek SSR")), which had assumed the position of capital of Soviet Central Asia, had risen to fourth place. [Minsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minsk "Minsk") ([Byelorussian SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byelorussian_SSR "Byelorussian SSR")) saw rapid growth during the 20th century, rising from the 32nd most populous in the union to the 7th.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Stronski-2010-189)[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-190)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-191)

### Women and fertility

[![Image 75](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/RIAN_archive_16735_Cosmonaut_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg/250px-RIAN_archive_16735_Cosmonaut_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_16735_Cosmonaut_Valentina_Tereshkova.jpg)

[Valentina Tereshkova](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova "Valentina Tereshkova"), the first woman in space, visiting the [Lvov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lvov "Lvov") confectionery, Ukrainian SSR, 1967

Under Lenin, the state made explicit commitments to promote the equality of men and women. Many early Russian feminists and ordinary Russian working women actively participated in the Revolution, and many more were affected by the events of that period and the new policies. Beginning in October 1918, Lenin's government liberalized divorce and abortion laws, decriminalized homosexuality (re-criminalized in 1932), permitted cohabitation, and ushered in a host of reforms.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-192) However, without [birth control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birth_control "Birth control"), the new system produced many broken marriages, as well as countless out-of-wedlock children.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-193) The epidemic of divorces and extramarital affairs created social hardships when Soviet leaders wanted people to concentrate their efforts on growing the economy. Giving women control over their fertility also led to a precipitous decline in the birth rate, perceived as a threat to their country's military power. By 1936, Stalin reversed most of the liberal laws, ushering in a [pronatalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natalism "Natalism") era that lasted for decades.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-194)

By 1917, Russia became the first [great power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_power "Great power") to grant women the right to vote.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-195) After heavy casualties in World Wars I and II, women outnumbered men in Russia by a 4:3 ratio;[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-196) this contributed to the larger role women played in Russian society compared to other great powers at the time.

### LGBT rights

The Soviet Union repressed [homosexuality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homosexuality "Homosexuality"). Even during the period when homosexuality was officially legal after the abolition of the Tsarist penal code criminalising it, Soviet courts attempted to repress non-traditional forms of sexuality, which were widely viewed by Russian revolutionaries as a form of capitalist decadence despite more liberal views on homosexuality from Soviet academic sexologists. After Stalin's consolidation of power, homosexuality became officially recriminalised in 1934.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-LauraEngelstein1995-197) The increased homophobia during this time interval was driven by the economic demands of the First Five-Year Plan, as well as the NKVD's view of homosexuals as "socially harmful elements", although even during this heightened period of repression, a clandestine homosexual subculture was able to persist.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-198) Homosexuality remained a criminal offence throughout the remainder of the Soviet Union's existence.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-LauraEngelstein1995-197)

### Education

[![Image 76](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Young_Pioneers_in_Kazakh_SSR.jpg/250px-Young_Pioneers_in_Kazakh_SSR.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Young_Pioneers_in_Kazakh_SSR.jpg)

[Young Pioneers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young_Pioneer_organization_of_the_Soviet_Union "Young Pioneer organization of the Soviet Union") at a Young Pioneer camp in the Kazakh SSR

[Anatoly Lunacharsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatoly_Lunacharsky "Anatoly Lunacharsky") became the first [People's Commissar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People%27s_Commissar "People's Commissar") for Education of Soviet Russia. In the beginning, the Soviet authorities placed great emphasis on the [elimination of illiteracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Likbez "Likbez"). All left-handed children were forced to write with their right hand in the Soviet school system.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-199)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-200)[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-201)[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-202) Literate people were automatically hired as teachers. [_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_] For a short period, quality was sacrificed for quantity. By 1940, Stalin could announce that illiteracy had been eliminated. Throughout the 1930s, [social mobility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_mobility "Social mobility") rose sharply, which has been attributed to reforms in education.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-203) In the aftermath of World War II, the country's educational system expanded dramatically, which had a tremendous effect. In the 1960s, nearly all children had access to education, the only exception being those living in remote areas. [Nikita Khrushchev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev "Nikita Khrushchev") tried to make education more accessible, making it clear to children that education was closely linked to the needs of society. Education also became important in giving rise to the [New Man](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Soviet_man "New Soviet man").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-204) Citizens directly entering the workforce had the constitutional right to a job and to free [vocational training](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vocational-technical_school "Vocational-technical school").

The [education system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_the_Soviet_Union "Education in the Soviet Union") was highly centralized and universally accessible to all citizens, with [affirmative action](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Affirmative_action "Affirmative action") for applicants from nations associated with [cultural backwardness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_backwardness "Cultural backwardness"). However, as part of a general [antisemitic policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antisemitism_in_the_Soviet_Union "Antisemitism in the Soviet Union"), an unofficial [Jewish quota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_quota "Jewish quota") was applied[_[when?](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Dates\_and\_numbers#Chronological\_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_] in the leading institutions of higher education by subjecting Jewish applicants to harsher entrance examinations.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-205)[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-206)[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-207)[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-208) The Brezhnev era also introduced a rule that required all university applicants to present a reference from the local [Komsomol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Komsomol "Komsomol") party secretary.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-209) According to statistics from 1986, the number of higher education students per the population of 10,000 was 181 for the USSR, compared to 517 for the US.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-210)

### Nationalities and ethnic groups

[![Image 77](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Samarkand-1981-0013.JPG/250px-Samarkand-1981-0013.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Samarkand-1981-0013.JPG)

People in [Samarkand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samarkand "Samarkand"), Uzbek SSR, 1981

[![Image 78](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mestia%2C_Svaneti._October_30%2C_1929.JPG/250px-Mestia%2C_Svaneti._October_30%2C_1929.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mestia,_Svaneti._October_30,_1929.JPG)

[Svaneti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Svaneti "Svaneti") man in [Mestia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mestia "Mestia"), Georgian SSR, 1929

The Soviet Union was an ethnically diverse country, with more than 100 distinct ethnic groups. The total population of the country was estimated at 293 million in 1991. According to a 1990 estimate, the majority of the population were [Russians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russians "Russians") (50.78%), followed by [Ukrainians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainians "Ukrainians") (15.45%) and [Uzbeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uzbeks "Uzbeks") (5.84%).[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-211) Overall, in 1989 the ethnic demography of the country showed that 69.8% was [East Slavic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Slavs "East Slavs"), 17.5% was [Turkic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkic_peoples "Turkic peoples"), 1.6% were [Armenians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenians "Armenians"), 1.6% were [Balts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balts "Balts"), 1.5% were [Uralic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uralic_peoples "Uralic peoples"), 1.5% were [Tajik](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tajiks "Tajiks"), 1.4% were [Georgian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgians "Georgians"), 1.2% were [Moldovan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moldovans "Moldovans") and 4.1% were of other various ethnic groups.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-212)

All citizens of the USSR had their own ethnic affiliation. The ethnicity of a person was chosen at the age of sixteen by the child's parents.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComrie19812-213) If the parents did not agree, the child was automatically assigned the ethnicity of the father. Partly due to Soviet policies, some of the smaller minority ethnic groups were considered part of larger ones, such as the [Mingrelians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mingrelians "Mingrelians") of [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic "Georgian Soviet Socialist Republic"), who were classified with the linguistically related [Georgians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgians "Georgians").[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-214) Some ethnic groups voluntarily assimilated, while others were brought in by force. Russians, [Belarusians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belarusians "Belarusians"), and Ukrainians, who were all East Slavic and Orthodox, shared close cultural, ethnic, and religious ties, while other groups did not. With multiple nationalities living in the same territory, [ethnic antagonisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnic_conflicts_in_the_Soviet_Union "Ethnic conflicts in the Soviet Union") developed over the years.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-215)[_[neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral\_point\_of\_view "Wikipedia:Neutral point of view") is [disputed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:Soviet\_Union "Talk:Soviet Union")_]

Members of various ethnicities participated in legislative bodies. Organs of power like the Politburo, the Secretariat of the Central Committee etc., were formally ethnically neutral, but in reality, ethnic Russians were overrepresented, although there were also non-Russian leaders in the [Soviet leadership](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_leaders_of_the_Soviet_Union "List of leaders of the Soviet Union"), such as [Joseph Stalin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Stalin "Joseph Stalin"), [Grigory Zinoviev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grigory_Zinoviev "Grigory Zinoviev"), [Nikolai Podgorny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolai_Podgorny "Nikolai Podgorny") or [Andrei Gromyko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrei_Gromyko "Andrei Gromyko"). During the Soviet era, a significant number of ethnic Russians and Ukrainians migrated to other Soviet republics, and many of them settled there. According to the last census in 1989, the Russian 'diaspora' in the Soviet republics had reached 25 million.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-216)

*   [![Image 79: Ethnographic map of the USSR, 1930](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Ethnic_map_USSR_1930.jpg/330px-Ethnic_map_USSR_1930.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ethnic_map_USSR_1930.jpg "Ethnographic map of the USSR, 1930")
