### Health

[![Image 87](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/RussianAbortionPoster.jpg/250px-RussianAbortionPoster.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RussianAbortionPoster.jpg)

An early Soviet-era poster discouraging unsafe abortion practices

In 1917, before the revolution, health conditions were significantly behind those of developed countries. As Lenin later noted, "Either the lice will defeat socialism, or socialism will defeat the lice".[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-217) The Soviet health care system was conceived by the [People's Commissariat for Health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People%27s_Commissariat_for_Health "People's Commissariat for Health") in 1918. Under the [Semashko model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semashko_model "Semashko model"), health care was to be controlled by the state and would be provided to its citizens free of charge, a revolutionary concept at the time. Article 42 of the [1977 Soviet Constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1977_Soviet_Constitution "1977 Soviet Constitution") gave all citizens the [right to health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_to_health "Right to health") protection and free access to any health institutions in the USSR. Before [Leonid Brezhnev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonid_Brezhnev "Leonid Brezhnev") became general secretary, the Soviet healthcare system was held in high esteem by many foreign specialists. This changed, however, from Brezhnev's accession and [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev")'s tenure as leader, during which the health care system was heavily criticized for many basic faults, such as the quality of service and the unevenness in its provision.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-218)[Minister of Health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Soviet_Union) "Ministry of Health (Soviet Union)")[Yevgeniy Chazov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yevgeniy_Chazov "Yevgeniy Chazov"), during the [19th Congress of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/19th_Congress_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "19th Congress of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union"), while highlighting such successes as having the most doctors and hospitals in the world, recognized the system's areas for improvement and felt that billions of [rubles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_ruble "Soviet ruble") were squandered.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-219)

After the revolution, life expectancy for all age groups went up. This statistic in itself was seen by some that the [socialist system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_mode_of_production "Socialist mode of production") was superior to the [capitalist system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitalist_system "Capitalist system"). These improvements continued into the 1960s when statistics indicated that the life expectancy briefly surpassed that of the United States. Life expectancy started to decline in the 1970s, possibly because of [alcohol abuse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcohol_consumption_in_Russia "Alcohol consumption in Russia"). At the same time, infant mortality began to rise. After 1974, the government stopped publishing statistics on the matter. This trend can be partly explained by the number of pregnancies rising drastically in the Asian part of the country where infant mortality was the highest while declining markedly in the more developed European part of the Soviet Union.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-220)

#### Dentistry

Soviet dental technology and dental health were considered notoriously bad. In 1991, the average 35-year-old had 12 to 14 cavities, fillings or missing teeth. Toothpaste was often not available, and toothbrushes did not conform to standards of modern dentistry.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-221)[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-222)

### Language

Under Lenin, the government gave small language groups their own writing systems.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComrie19813%E2%80%934-223) The development of these writing systems was highly successful, even though some flaws were detected. During the later days of the USSR, countries with the same [multilingual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multilingual "Multilingual") situation implemented similar policies. A serious problem when creating these writing systems was that the languages differed [dialectally](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dialect "Dialect") greatly from each other.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComrie19814-224) When a language had been given a writing system and appeared in a notable publication, it would attain 'official language' status. There were many minority languages which never received their own writing system; therefore, their speakers were forced to have a [second language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_language "Second language").[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComrie198125-225) There are examples where the government retreated from this policy, most notably under Stalin where education was discontinued in languages that were not widespread. These languages were then assimilated into another language, mostly Russian.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComrie198126-226) During World War II, some minority languages were banned, and their speakers accused of collaborating with the enemy.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComrie198127-227)

As the most widely spoken of the Soviet Union's many languages, Russian _de facto_ functioned as an official language, as the 'language of interethnic communication' (Russian: язык межнационального общения), but only assumed the _[de jure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De\_jure "De jure")_ status as the official national language in 1990.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-228)

### Religion

[![Image 88](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg/250px-Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg)

Cover of [Bezbozhnik](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bezbozhnik_(magazine) "Bezbozhnik (magazine)") in 1929, magazine of the Society of the Godless. The first five-year plan of the Soviet Union is shown crushing the gods of the [Abrahamic religions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abrahamic_religions "Abrahamic religions").

[![Image 89](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg/250px-Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg)

The [Cathedral of Christ the Saviour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour "Cathedral of Christ the Saviour") in Moscow during its demolition in 1931

[![Image 90](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Sennaia-1900.jpg/250px-Sennaia-1900.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sennaia-1900.jpg)

The [Saviour Church on Sennaya Square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saviour_Church_on_Sennaya_Square "Saviour Church on Sennaya Square") in Leningrad was one of many notable church buildings destroyed during the [Khrushchev Thaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khrushchev_Thaw "Khrushchev Thaw").

[![Image 91](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Hujum.png/250px-Hujum.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hujum.png)

A [paranja](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranja "Paranja") burning ceremony in the [Uzbek SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uzbek_SSR "Uzbek SSR") as part of Soviet [Hujum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hujum "Hujum") policies

[![Image 92](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/U.S.S.R.-Major_religious_groups._4-61._LOC_75694086.jpg/250px-U.S.S.R.-Major_religious_groups._4-61._LOC_75694086.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:U.S.S.R.-Major_religious_groups._4-61._LOC_75694086.jpg)

Major religious groups in the Soviet Union as published by the [CIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIA "CIA")

[Christianity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity "Christianity") and [Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam "Islam") had the highest number of adherents among the religious citizens.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Eaton,_Katherine_Bliss-2004-229)[Eastern Christianity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Christianity "Eastern Christianity") predominated among Christians, with Russia's traditional [Russian Orthodox Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church "Russian Orthodox Church") being the largest [Christian denomination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_denomination "Christian denomination"). About 90% of the Soviet Union's Muslims were [Sunnis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni "Sunni"), with [Shias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shias "Shias") being concentrated in the [Azerbaijan SSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azerbaijan_SSR "Azerbaijan SSR").[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Eaton,_Katherine_Bliss-2004-229) Smaller groups included [Roman Catholics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Catholics "Roman Catholics"), Jews, [Buddhists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buddhists "Buddhists"), and a variety of [Protestant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protestant "Protestant") denominations (especially [Baptists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baptists "Baptists") and [Lutherans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lutherans "Lutherans")).[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Eaton,_Katherine_Bliss-2004-229)

Religious influence had been strong in the Russian Empire. The Russian Orthodox Church enjoyed a privileged status as the church of the monarchy and took part in carrying out official state functions.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-230) The immediate period following the establishment of the Soviet state included a struggle against the Orthodox Church, which the revolutionaries considered an ally of the former [ruling classes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruling_class "Ruling class").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Simon_1974-231)

In Soviet law, the 'freedom to hold religious services' was constitutionally guaranteed, although the ruling Communist Party regarded religion as incompatible with the [Marxist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxist "Marxist") spirit of [scientific materialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_materialism "Scientific materialism").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Simon_1974-231) In practice, the Soviet system subscribed to a narrow interpretation of this right, and in fact used a range of official measures to discourage religion and curb the activities of religious groups.[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Simon_1974-231)

The 1918 [Council of People's Commissars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Council_of_People%27s_Commissars "Council of People's Commissars") decree establishing the Russian SFSR as a secular state also decreed that 'the teaching of religion in all [places] where subjects of general instruction are taught, is forbidden. Citizens may teach and may be taught religion privately.'[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-232) Among further restrictions, those adopted in 1929 included express prohibitions on a range of church activities, including meetings for organized [Bible study](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bible_study_(Christian) "Bible study (Christian)").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Simon_1974-231) Both Christian and non-Christian establishments were shut down by the thousands in the 1920s and 1930s. By 1940, as many as 90% of the churches, synagogues, and mosques that had been operating in 1917 were closed; the majority of them were demolished or re-purposed for state needs with little concern for their historic and cultural value.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-233)

More than 85,000 Orthodox priests were shot in 1937 alone.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-234) Only a twelfth of the Russian Orthodox Church's priests were left functioning in their parishes by 1941.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-235) In the period between 1927 and 1940, the number of Orthodox Churches in Russia fell from 29,584 to less than 500 (1.7%).[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-236)

The Soviet Union was officially a [secular state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secular_state "Secular state"),[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-237)[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-238) but a 'government-sponsored program of forced conversion to [atheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxist-Leninist_atheism "Marxist-Leninist atheism")' was conducted under the doctrine of [state atheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_atheism "State atheism").[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-239)[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-240)[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-241) The government targeted religions based on state interests, and while most organized religions were never outlawed, religious property was confiscated, believers were harassed, and religion was ridiculed while atheism was propagated in schools.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-242) In 1925, the government founded the [League of Militant Atheists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/League_of_Militant_Atheists "League of Militant Atheists") to intensify the propaganda campaign.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-243) Accordingly, although personal expressions of religious faith were not explicitly banned, a strong sense of social stigma was imposed on them by the formal structures and mass media, and it was generally considered unacceptable for members of certain professions (teachers, state bureaucrats, soldiers) to be openly religious. While persecution accelerated following Stalin's rise to power, a revival of Orthodoxy was fostered by the government during World War II and the Soviet authorities sought to control the Russian Orthodox Church rather than liquidate it. During the first five years of Soviet power, the Bolsheviks executed 28 Russian Orthodox bishops and over 1,200 Russian Orthodox priests. Many others were imprisoned or exiled. Believers were harassed and persecuted. Most seminaries were closed, and the publication of most religious material was prohibited. By 1941, only 500 churches remained open out of about 54,000 in existence before World War I.

Convinced that religious [anti-Sovietism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Sovietism "Anti-Sovietism") had become a thing of the past, and with the looming threat of war, the Stalin administration began shifting to a more moderate religion policy in the late 1930s.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Janz_1998-244) Soviet religious establishments overwhelmingly rallied to support the war effort during World War II. Amid other accommodations to religious faith after the German invasion, churches were reopened. [Radio Moscow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_Moscow "Radio Moscow") began broadcasting a religious hour, and a historic meeting between Stalin and Orthodox Church leader [Patriarch Sergius of Moscow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patriarch_Sergius_of_Moscow "Patriarch Sergius of Moscow") was held in 1943. Stalin had the support of the majority of the religious people in the USSR even through the late 1980s.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Janz_1998-244) The general tendency of this period was an increase in religious activity among believers of all faiths.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-245)

Under [Nikita Khrushchev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev "Nikita Khrushchev"), the state leadership clashed with the churches in 1958–1964, a period when [atheism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atheism "Atheism") was emphasized in the educational curriculum, and numerous state publications promoted atheistic views.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Janz_1998-244) During this period, the number of churches fell from 20,000 to 10,000 from 1959 to 1965, and the number of synagogues dropped from 500 to 97.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Victor_Swoboda-1990-246) The number of working mosques also declined, falling from 1,500 to 500 within a decade.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Victor_Swoboda-1990-246)

Religious institutions remained monitored by the Soviet government, but churches, synagogues, temples, and mosques were all given more leeway in the [Brezhnev era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brezhnev_era "Brezhnev era").[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-247) Official relations between the Orthodox Church and the government again warmed to the point that the Brezhnev government twice honored Orthodox Patriarch [Alexy I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexy_I "Alexy I") with the [Order of the Red Banner of Labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_the_Red_Banner_of_Labour "Order of the Red Banner of Labour").[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-248) A poll conducted by Soviet authorities in 1982 recorded 20% of the Soviet population as 'active religious believers.'[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-249)

Culture
-------

The 'Enthusiast's March', a 1930s song famous in the Soviet Union

[![Image 93](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vladimir_Vysotsky.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Vysotsky.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vladimir_Vysotsky.jpg)

Soviet singer-songwriter, poet and actor [Vladimir Vysotsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Vysotsky "Vladimir Vysotsky") in 1979

The culture of the Soviet Union evolved through several stages during its existence. During the first decade following the revolution, there was relative freedom and artists experimented with several different styles to find a distinctive Soviet style of art. Lenin wanted art to be accessible to the Russian people. On the other hand, hundreds of intellectuals, writers, and artists were exiled or executed, and their work banned, such as [Nikolay Gumilyov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolay_Gumilyov "Nikolay Gumilyov") who was shot for alleged conspiracy against the Bolsheviks, and [Yevgeny Zamyatin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yevgeny_Zamyatin "Yevgeny Zamyatin").[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-250)

The government encouraged a variety of trends. In art and literature, numerous schools, some traditional and others radically experimental, proliferated. Communist writers [Maxim Gorky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxim_Gorky "Maxim Gorky") and [Vladimir Mayakovsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Mayakovsky "Vladimir Mayakovsky") were active during this time. As a means of influencing a largely illiterate society, films received encouragement from the state, and much of director [Sergei Eisenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergei_Eisenstein "Sergei Eisenstein")'s best work dates from this period.

During Stalin's rule, the Soviet culture was characterized by the rise and domination of the government-imposed style of [socialist realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_realism "Socialist realism"), with all other trends being severely repressed, with rare exceptions, such as [Mikhail Bulgakov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Bulgakov "Mikhail Bulgakov")'s works. Many writers were imprisoned and killed.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-251)

Following the [Khrushchev Thaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khrushchev_Thaw "Khrushchev Thaw"), censorship was diminished. During this time, a distinctive period of Soviet culture developed, characterized by conformist public life and an intense focus on personal life. Greater experimentation in art forms was again permissible, resulting in the production of more sophisticated and subtly critical work. The government loosened its emphasis on socialist realism; thus, for instance, many protagonists of the novels of author [Yury Trifonov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yury_Trifonov "Yury Trifonov") concerned themselves with problems of daily life rather than with building socialism. Underground dissident literature, known as _[samizdat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samizdat "Samizdat")_, developed during this late period. In architecture, the Khrushchev era mostly focused on functional design as opposed to the highly decorated style of Stalin's epoch. In music, in response to the increasing popularity of forms of popular music like [jazz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jazz "Jazz") in the West, many jazz orchestras were permitted throughout the USSR, notably the Melodiya Ensemble, named after the principle record label in the USSR.

In the second half of the 1980s, Gorbachev's policies of _[perestroika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perestroika "Perestroika")_ and _[glasnost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasnost "Glasnost")_ significantly expanded [freedom of expression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_of_expression "Freedom of expression") throughout the country in the media and the press.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-252)

Sport
-----

[![Image 94](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/RR5110-0098R.png/250px-RR5110-0098R.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RR5110-0098R.png)

[Valeri Kharlamov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valeri_Kharlamov "Valeri Kharlamov") represented the Soviet Union at 11 [Ice Hockey World Championships](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_Hockey_World_Championships "Ice Hockey World Championships"), winning eight gold medals, two silvers and one bronze.

In summer of 1923 in Moscow was established the [Proletarian Sports Society "Dynamo"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamo_Sports_Club "Dynamo Sports Club") as a sports organization of Soviet secret police [Cheka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cheka "Cheka").

On 13 July 1925 the [Central Committee of the Russian Communist Party (Bolsheviks)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Committee_of_the_Russian_Communist_Party_(Bolsheviks) "Central Committee of the Russian Communist Party (Bolsheviks)") adopted a statement "About the party's tasks in sphere of physical culture". In the statement was determined the role of physical culture in Soviet society and the party's tasks in political leadership of physical culture movement in the country.

The [Soviet Olympic Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Olympic_Committee "Soviet Olympic Committee") formed on 21 April 1951, and the [IOC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IOC "IOC") recognized the new body in its [45th session](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_IOC_meetings "List of IOC meetings"). In the same year, when the Soviet representative Konstantin Andrianov became an IOC member, the USSR officially joined the [Olympic Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympic_Movement "Olympic Movement"). The [1952 Summer Olympics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1952_Summer_Olympics "1952 Summer Olympics") in Helsinki thus became first Olympic Games for Soviet athletes. The Soviet Union was the biggest rival to the United States at the Summer Olympics, winning [six of its nine appearances at the games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union_at_the_Olympics#Medals_by_Summer_Games "Soviet Union at the Olympics") and also topping the medal tally at the Winter Olympics six times. The Soviet Union's Olympics success has been attributed to its large investment in sports to demonstrate its superpower image and political influence on a global stage.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Benson-253)

The [Soviet Union national ice hockey team](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union_national_ice_hockey_team "Soviet Union national ice hockey team") won nearly every [world championship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_Hockey_World_Championships "Ice Hockey World Championships") and [Olympic tournament](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_Olympic_Games "Ice hockey at the Olympic Games") between 1954 and 1991 and never failed to medal in any [International Ice Hockey Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Ice_Hockey_Federation "International Ice Hockey Federation") (IIHF) tournament in which they competed.

The Soviet Olympic team was notorious for skirting the edge of amateur rules. All Soviet athletes held some nominal jobs, but were in fact state-sponsored and trained full-time. According to many experts, that gave the Soviet Union a huge advantage over the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") and other Western countries, whose athletes were students or real amateurs.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-254)[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-255) Indeed, the Soviet Union monopolized the top place in the medal standings after 1968, and, until its collapse, placed second only once, in the [1984 Winter games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1984_Winter_Olympics "1984 Winter Olympics"), after another Eastern bloc nation, the [GDR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GDR "GDR"). Amateur rules were relaxed only in the late 1980s and were almost completely abolished in the 1990s, after the [fall of the USSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_the_USSR "Fall of the USSR").[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Benson-253)[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-256)

According to British journalist [Andrew Jennings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_Jennings "Andrew Jennings"), a [KGB](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/KGB "KGB") colonel stated that the agency's officers had posed as anti-doping authorities from the [International Olympic Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee "International Olympic Committee") (IOC) to undermine [doping tests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doping_test "Doping test") and that Soviet athletes were "rescued with [these] tremendous efforts".[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-257)[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-258) Documents obtained in 2016 revealed the Soviet Union's plans for a statewide doping system in track and field in preparation for the [1984 Summer Olympics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics "1984 Summer Olympics") in Los Angeles. Dated prior to the country's decision to boycott the Games, the document detailed the existing steroids operations of the program, along with suggestions for further enhancements.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-259)

Environment
-----------

[![Image 95](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Karabash_mountain.jpg/250px-Karabash_mountain.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Karabash_mountain.jpg)

Landscape near [Karabash, Chelyabinsk Oblast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karabash,_Chelyabinsk_Oblast "Karabash, Chelyabinsk Oblast"), an area that was previously covered with forests until acid rainfall from a nearby copper smelter killed all vegetation

[![Image 96](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/AralSea1989_2014.jpg/250px-AralSea1989_2014.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:AralSea1989_2014.jpg)

One of the many impacts of the approach to the environment in the USSR and post-Soviet states is the [Aral Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aral_Sea "Aral Sea"). (See status in 1989 and 2014)[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-260)

Neighbouring countries were aware of the high levels of pollution in the Soviet Union[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-261)[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-262) but after the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union") it was discovered that its environmental problems were greater than what the Soviet authorities admitted.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-263) The Soviet Union was the world's second largest producer of harmful emissions. In 1988, total emissions in the Soviet Union were about 79% of those in the United States. But since the Soviet [GNP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GNP "GNP") was only 54% of that of the United States, this means that the Soviet Union generated 1.5 times more pollution than the United States per unit of GNP.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-264)

The [Chernobyl disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster "Chernobyl disaster") in the Ukrainian SSR in 1986 was the first major accident at a civilian [nuclear power plant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant "Nuclear power plant").[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-265)[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-266)[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-267) Unparalleled in the world, it resulted in a large number of radioactive isotopes being released into the atmosphere. Radioactive doses were scattered relatively far.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-268) Although long-term effects of the accident were unknown, 4,000 new cases of thyroid cancer which resulted from the accident's contamination were reported at the time of the accident, but this led to a relatively low number of deaths (WHO data, 2005).[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-269) Another major radioactive accident was the [Kyshtym disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kyshtym_disaster "Kyshtym disaster").[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-270)

The [Kola Peninsula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kola_Peninsula "Kola Peninsula") was one of the places with major problems.[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-271) Around the industrial cities of [Monchegorsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monchegorsk "Monchegorsk") and [Norilsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norilsk "Norilsk"), where [nickel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel "Nickel"), for example, is mined, all forests have been destroyed by contamination, while the northern and other parts of Russia have been affected by emissions.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-272) During the 1990s, people in the West were also interested in the radioactive hazards of nuclear facilities, decommissioned [nuclear submarines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_submarine "Nuclear submarine"), and the processing of [nuclear waste](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_waste "Nuclear waste") or [spent nuclear fuel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel "Spent nuclear fuel").[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-273)[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-274) It was also known in the early 1990s that the USSR had transported radioactive material to the [Barents Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barents_Sea "Barents Sea") and [Kara Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kara_Sea "Kara Sea"), which was later confirmed by the Russian parliament. The crash of the [K-141 Kursk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-141_Kursk "K-141 Kursk") submarine in 2000 in the west further raised concerns.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-275) In the past, there were accidents involving submarines [K-19](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-19 "Soviet submarine K-19"), [K-8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-8 "Soviet submarine K-8"), a [K-129](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-129 "K-129"), [K-27](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27 "Soviet submarine K-27"), [K-219](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-219 "Soviet submarine K-219") and [K-278 Komsomolets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-278_Komsomolets "K-278 Komsomolets").[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-276)[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-277)[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-278)[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-279)

Legacy
------

The legacy of the USSR remains a controversial topic. The socio-economic nature of [communist states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_state "Communist state") such as the USSR, especially under Stalin, has also been much debated, varyingly being labelled a form of [bureaucratic collectivism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bureaucratic_collectivism "Bureaucratic collectivism"), [state capitalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_capitalism "State capitalism"), [state socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_socialism "State socialism"), or a totally unique [mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_of_production "Mode of production").[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-280) The USSR implemented a broad range of policies over a long period of time, with a large amount of conflicting policies being implemented by different leaders. Some have a positive view of it whilst others are critical towards the country, calling it a repressive [oligarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oligarchy "Oligarchy").[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-281) The opinions on the USSR are complex and have changed over time, with different generations having different views on the matter as well as on Soviet policies corresponding to separate time periods during its history.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-282)

[![Image 97](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Stamp_of_Moldova_md383.jpg/250px-Stamp_of_Moldova_md383.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stamp_of_Moldova_md383.jpg)

2001 stamp of [Moldova](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moldova "Moldova") shows [Yuri Gagarin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin "Yuri Gagarin"), the first human in space.

Western academicians published various analyses of the post-Soviet states' development, claiming that the dissolution was followed by a severe drop in economic and social conditions in these countries,[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-283)[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-284) including a rapid increase in poverty,[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Scheidel-2017-285)[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-286)[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-287)[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-288) crime,[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-289) corruption,[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-290)[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-291) unemployment,[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-292)[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-293) homelessness,[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-294)[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-295) rates of disease,[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-296)[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-297)[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-298) infant mortality and domestic violence,[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-299) as well as demographic losses,[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-300) income inequality and the rise of an [oligarchical class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_oligarchs "Russian oligarchs"),[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-301)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Scheidel-2017-285) along with decreases in calorie intake, life expectancy, adult literacy, and income.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-302) Between 1988–1989 and 1993–1995, the [Gini ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gini_ratio "Gini ratio") (a measure of inequality) increased by an average of 9 percentage points for all former Soviet republics.[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Scheidel-2017-285) According to Western analysis, the economic shocks that accompanied wholesale [privatization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privatization "Privatization") were associated with sharp increases in mortality,[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-303) Russia, Kazakhstan, Latvia, Lithuania, and Estonia saw a tripling of unemployment and a 42% increase in male death rates between 1991 and 1994,[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-304)[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-305) and in the following decades, only five or six of the post-communist states are on a path to joining the wealthy capitalist West while most are falling behind, some to such an extent that it will take over fifty years to catch up to where they were before the fall of the Soviet Bloc.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-306)[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-307) As of 2011, the experience of the former Soviet republics was mixed, with some having recovered in terms of [gross domestic product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product") and others not.[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-308) There are large wealth disparities, and many post-soviet economies are described as oligarchic.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-309)

Since the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union"), annual polling by the [Levada Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levada_Center "Levada Center") has shown that over 50% of Russia's population regretted this event, with the only exception to this being in 2012 when support for the Soviet Union dipped below 50 percent.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-%D0%9D%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B3%D0%B8%D1%8F_%D0%BF%D0%BE_%D0%A1%D0%A1%D0%A1%D0%A0-2018-310) A 2018 poll showed that 66% of [Russians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russians "Russians") regretted the fall of the Soviet Union, setting a 15-year record, and the majority of these regretting opinions came from people older than 55.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-%D0%9D%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B3%D0%B8%D1%8F_%D0%BF%D0%BE_%D0%A1%D0%A1%D0%A1%D0%A0-2018-310)[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-311) In 2020, polls conducted by the Levada Center found that 75% of Russians agreed that the Soviet era was the greatest era in their country's history.[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-312)

According to the New Russia Barometer (NRB) polls by the Centre for the Study of Public Policy, 50% of Russian respondents reported a positive impression of the Soviet Union in 1991.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Rose-2011-313) This increased to about 75% of NRB respondents in 2000, dropping slightly to 71% in 2009.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Rose-2011-313) Throughout the 2000s, an average of 32% of NRB respondents supported the restoration of the Soviet Union.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Rose-2011-313)

In a 2021 poll, a record 70% of Russians indicated they had a mostly/very favourable view of [Joseph Stalin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Stalin "Joseph Stalin").[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-314) In [Armenia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenia "Armenia"), 12% of respondents said the USSR collapse did good, while 66% said it did harm. In [Kyrgyzstan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kyrgyzstan "Kyrgyzstan"), 16% of respondents said the collapse of the USSR did good, while 61% said it did harm.[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-315) In a 2018 [Rating Sociological Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sociological_group_%22RATING%22 "Sociological group \"RATING\"") poll, 47% of [Ukrainian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainians "Ukrainians") respondents had a positive opinion of Soviet leader [Leonid Brezhnev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonid_Brezhnev "Leonid Brezhnev"), who ruled the Soviet Union from 1964 to 1982, while viewing [Lenin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin "Lenin"), Stalin, and [Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorbachev "Gorbachev") very negatively.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-316) A 2021 poll conducted by the Levada Center found that 49% of Russians prefer the USSR's political system, while 18% prefer the current political system and 16% would prefer a [Western democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_democracy "Western democracy"). A further 62% of people polled preferred the Soviet system of central planning, while 24% prefer a market-based system.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-317) According to the Levada Center's polls, the primary reasons cited for Soviet nostalgia are the advantages of the shared economic union between the Soviet republics, including perceived financial stability.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-The_Washington_Post-2016-318) This was referenced by up to 53% of respondents in 2016.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-The_Washington_Post-2016-318) At least 43% also lamented the loss of the Soviet Union's global political superpower status.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-The_Washington_Post-2016-318) About 31% cited the loss of social trust and capital.[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-319) The remainder of the respondents cited a mix of reasons ranging from practical travel difficulties to a sense of national displacement.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-The_Washington_Post-2016-318)

The 1941–1945 period of World War II is still known in Russia as the '[Great Patriotic War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Patriotic_War_(term) "Great Patriotic War (term)")'. The war became a topic of great importance in cinema, literature, history lessons at school, the mass media, and the arts. As a result of the [massive losses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_the_Soviet_Union "World War II casualties of the Soviet Union") suffered by the military and civilians during the conflict, [Victory Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victory_Day_(9_May) "Victory Day (9 May)") celebrated on 9 May is still one of the most important and emotional dates in Russia.[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-320) Catherine Wanner asserts that Victory Day commemorations are a vehicle for Soviet nostalgia, as they "kept alive a mythology of Soviet grandeur, of solidarity among the _Sovietskii narod_, and of a sense of self as citizen of a superpower state".[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-321)

Russian Victory Day parades are organized annually in most cities, with the central military parade taking place in [Moscow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow "Moscow") (just as during the Soviet times).[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-322)[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-323) Additionally, the recently introduced [Immortal Regiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immortal_Regiment "Immortal Regiment") on 9 May sees millions of Russians carry the portraits of their relatives who fought in the war.[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-324) Russia also [retains other Soviet holidays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Russia "Public holidays in Russia"), such as the [Defender of the Fatherland Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Defender_of_the_Fatherland_Day "Defender of the Fatherland Day") (23 February), [International Women's Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day "International Women's Day") (8 March), and [International Workers' Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day#Russia "International Workers' Day").[[300]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-325)

### In the former Soviet republics

[![Image 98](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/2018-05-09._%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%8C_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B5%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_f171.jpg/250px-2018-05-09._%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%8C_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B5%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_f171.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2018-05-09._%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%8C_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B5%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_f171.jpg)

People in the [Donetsk People's Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donetsk_People%27s_Republic "Donetsk People's Republic") celebrate the annual [Victory Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victory_Day_(9_May) "Victory Day (9 May)") over [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany "Nazi Germany"), 9 May 2018.

[![Image 99](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/2014-03-08._%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B3_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_006.jpg/250px-2014-03-08._%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B3_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_006.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2014-03-08._%D0%9C%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B3_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_006.jpg)

Protest against [Ukrainian decommunization policies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decommunization_in_Ukraine "Decommunization in Ukraine") in Donetsk, 2014. The red banner reads, "Our homeland USSR".

In some post-Soviet republics, there is a more negative view of the USSR, although there is no unanimity on the matter.[[301]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-Budryte-326) In large part due to the [Holodomor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holodomor "Holodomor"), ethnic [Ukrainians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainians "Ukrainians") have a negative view of the Soviet Union.[[302]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-327)[Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_language_in_Ukraine "Russian language in Ukraine")-speaking Ukrainians of Ukraine's southern and eastern regions have a more positive view of the USSR. In some countries with internal conflict, there is also nostalgia for the USSR, especially for [refugees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refugee "Refugee") of the [post-Soviet conflicts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-Soviet_conflicts "Post-Soviet conflicts") who have been forced to flee their homes and have been displaced. The many Russian enclaves in the former USSR republics such as [Transnistria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transnistria "Transnistria") have in a general a positive remembrance of it.[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-328)

### By the political left

The left's view of the USSR is complex. While some leftists regard the USSR as an example of state capitalism or that it was an oligarchical state, other leftists admire [Vladimir Lenin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin "Vladimir Lenin") and the [Russian Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Revolution "Russian Revolution").[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-329)[Council communists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Council_communists "Council communists") generally view the USSR as failing to create [class consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_consciousness "Class consciousness"), turning into a corrupt state in which the elite controlled society.

[Trotskyists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trotskyists "Trotskyists") believe that the ascendancy of the Stalinist bureaucracy ensured a [degenerated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degenerated_workers%27_state "Degenerated workers' state") or [deformed workers' state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deformed_workers%27_state "Deformed workers' state"), where the capitalist elite have been replaced by an unaccountable bureaucratic elite and there is no true democracy or workers' control of industry.[[305]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-330) In particular, American Trotskyist [David North](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_North_(socialist) "David North (socialist)") noted that the generation of [bureaucrats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nomenklatura "Nomenklatura") that rose to power under Stalin's tutelage presided over the [stagnation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stagnation_of_the_Soviet_Union "Stagnation of the Soviet Union") and [breakdown](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union") of the Soviet Union.[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-331)

Many [anti-Stalinist leftists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Stalinist_left "Anti-Stalinist left") such as anarchists are extremely critical of Soviet authoritarianism and [repression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nabat#Decline "Nabat"). Much of the criticism it receives is centered around [massacres in the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_massacres_in_the_Soviet_Union "List of massacres in the Soviet Union"), the centralized [hierarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hierarchy "Hierarchy") present in the USSR and mass [political repression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_repression_in_the_Soviet_Union "Political repression in the Soviet Union") as well as violence towards government critics and [political dissidents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_dissidents "Soviet dissidents") such as other leftists. Critics also point towards its failure to implement any substantial [worker cooperatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Worker_cooperatives "Worker cooperatives") or implementing worker liberation, as well as corruption and the Soviet authoritarian nature.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

Anarchists are also critical of the country, labeling the Soviet system as _[red fascism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red\_fascism "Red fascism")_. Factors contributing to the anarchist animosity towards the USSR included the Soviet destruction of the [Makhnovist movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Makhnovist_movement "Makhnovist movement") after an initial alliance, the suppression of the anarchist [Kronstadt rebellion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kronstadt_rebellion "Kronstadt rebellion"), and the defeat of the rival anarchist factions by the Soviet-supported Communist faction during the [Spanish Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War "Spanish Civil War").[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-332)

[Maoists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maoists "Maoists") also have a mixed opinion on the USSR, viewing it negatively during the [Sino-Soviet Split](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sino-Soviet_Split "Sino-Soviet Split") and denouncing it as revisionist and reverted to capitalism. The Chinese government in 1963 articulated its criticism of the USSR's system and promoted China's ideological line as an alternative.[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-333)[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-334)

After the dissolution of the Soviet Union, the [Japanese Communist Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_Communist_Party "Japanese Communist Party") (JCP) released a press statement titled "We welcome the end of a party which embodied the historical evil of [great power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_power "Great power")[chauvinism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chauvinism "Chauvinism") and [hegemonism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hegemonism "Hegemonism")".[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-335)

[Noam Chomsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noam_Chomsky "Noam Chomsky") called the collapse of the Soviet Union "a small victory for socialism, not only because of the fall of one of the most anti-socialist states in the world, where working people had fewer rights than in the West, but also because it freed the term 'socialism' from the burden of being associated in the propaganda systems of East and West with Soviet tyranny—for the East, in order to benefit from the aura of authentic socialism, for the West, in order to demonize the concept."[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-336) Some scholars on the left have posited that the end of the Soviet Union and [communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communism "Communism") as a global force allowed [neoliberal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoliberal "Neoliberal")[capitalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitalism "Capitalism") to become a global system, which has resulted in rising [economic inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_inequality "Economic inequality").[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-337)[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-338)[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-339)[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_note-340)

23.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_ref-27)**As of 1989, the countries that bordered the Soviet Union were: [Norway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norway "Norway") and [Finland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finland "Finland") to the northwest; [Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic "Polish People's Republic"), [Czechoslovakia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czechoslovak_Socialist_Republic "Czechoslovak Socialist Republic"), [Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_People%27s_Republic "Hungarian People's Republic") and [Romania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Romania "Socialist Republic of Romania") to the west; [Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkey "Turkey") and [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran") to the southwest; [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan "Democratic Republic of Afghanistan") and [Mongolia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Republic "Mongolian People's Republic") to the south; [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China") and [North Korea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Korea "North Korea") to the southeast. The Soviet Union also shared [maritime boundaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maritime_boundaries "Maritime boundaries") with Japan (which was bordered to the south until 1945) and the United States.

114.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union#cite_ref-139)**Rosefielde, Steven (1996). "Stalinism in Post-Communist Perspective: New Evidence on Killings, Forced Labour and Economic Growth in the 1930s". _Europe-Asia Studies_. **48** (6): 956–987. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1080/09668139608412393](https://doi.org/10.1080%2F09668139608412393). [JSTOR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier) "JSTOR (identifier)")[152635](https://www.jstor.org/stable/152635). The new evidence shows that administrative command planning and Stalin's forced industrialization strategies failed in the 1930s and beyond. The economic miracle chronicled in official hagiographies and until recently faithfully recounted in Western textbooks has no basis in fact. It is the statistical artefact not of index number relativity (the Gerschenkron effect) but of misapplying to the calculation of growth cost prices that do not accurately measure competitive value. The standard of living declined during the 1930s in response to Stalin's despotism, and after a brief improvement following his death, lapsed into stagnation. Glasnost and post-communist revelations interpreted as a whole thus provide no basis for Getty, Rittersporn & Zemskov's relatively favorable characterization of the methods, economic achievements and human costs of Stalinism. The evidence demonstrates that the suppression of markets and the oppression of vast segments of the population were economically counterproductive and humanly calamitous, just as anyone conversant with classical economic theory should have expected.

The collapse of communism, then, opened the entire world to capitalist penetration, shrank the imaginative and ideological space in which opposition to capitalist thought and practices might incubate, and impelled those who remained leftists to redefine their radicalism in alternative terms, which turned out to be those that capitalist systems could more, rather than less, easily manage. This was the moment when neoliberalism in the United States went from being a political movement to a political order.
